Let's try to find a characterization. We need to understand the iteration f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. For N composite with at least 3 proper divisors.

Let's denote the three largest proper divisors. As argued, they are N/d2, N/d3, N/d4 where d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest proper divisors >1. So f(N) = N*(1/d2+1/d3+1/d4). Since d2,d3,d4 are divisors, and they are the smallest divisors >1, we have d2 = smallest prime factor, call it p. Then d3 is either p^2 (if p^2 divides N) or the next smallest prime factor q (if N has another prime factor). Similarly d4 is either p^3, or p*q, or q^2, etc., depending on the factorization.

We can try to analyze the iteration in terms of the prime factorization. We need to find all N such that the sequence never hits a prime or a prime square. Since the sequence is defined for all n, and each term must have at least 3 proper divisors, we need that the orbit stays in the set of numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. This is a kind of dynamical system on the set of positive integers. Possibly the only such numbers are those that are multiples of 6 and also not divisible by 5? But 42 works and is divisible by 7, not 5. 66 works, divisible by 11, not 5. 78 works, divisible by 13, not 5. 102 works, divisible by 17, not 5. 114 works, divisible by 19, not 5. 126 works, divisible by 7, not 5. 138 works, divisible by 23, not 5. 162 works, only 2,3. 186 works, divisible by 31. 198 works, divisible by 11. 216 works, divisible by 2,3 only. 234 works, divisible by 13. So all these numbers are not divisible by 5. But 30 is divisible by 5 and fails. 60 fails. 90 fails. 150 fails. 210 fails. So maybe the condition is that N is not divisible by any prime >3? That would be 3-smooth numbers. But 42 is not 3-smooth because it has factor 7, but it works. So that's not it.

Maybe the answer is that a1 must be a multiple of 6, and also not divisible by any prime of the form 2 mod 3? Not sure.

Let's analyze the condition for a number N to be a fixed point of f, i.e., f(N)=N. That means sum of three largest proper divisors equals N. Let the three largest proper divisors be x, y, z with x>y>z. Then x+y+z=N. Also note that these three are N divided by the three smallest proper divisors >1. So we have N = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4, i.e., 1/d2+1/d3+1/d4=1. So d2,d3,d4 are positive integers >1 that are divisors of N and satisfy 1/d2+1/d3+1/d4=1. This is a well-known Diophantine equation: The only solutions in integers >1 (order not matter) are (2,3,6) and (2,4,4) and (3,3,3)? Let's check: 1/2+1/3+1/6=1. 1/2+1/4+1/4=1. 1/3+1/3+1/3=1. So the possible sets are {2,3,6}, {2,4,4}, {3,3,3}. Also possibly (2,5,? 1/2+1/5+1/? =1 => 1/? = 1-0.5-0.2=0.3 => ?=10/3 not integer. So only these.

Thus if N is a fixed point, then the three smallest proper divisors >1 must be a permutation of {2,3,6} or {2,4,4} or {3,3,3}. But note: d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest proper divisors >1. So for N to be a fixed point, it must have 2,3,6 as its three smallest proper divisors? Actually if the set is {2,4,4}, then d2=2, d3=4, d4=4. That means N is divisible by 2 and 4, so N is divisible by 4, but not necessarily by 3? If N is divisible by 4, then 2 is a divisor. Then the next smallest divisor >1 could be 4 (if N is not divisible by 3). Then d3=4, d4=4 (since 4 is a divisor, but we need three smallest, so if N has only one divisor between 2 and 4? Actually the three smallest proper divisors >1: they are the first three divisors after 1. So if N is divisible by 4 but not by 3, then the divisors are 1,2,4,... So d2=2, d3=4, d4 could be something else, but if N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor, but we need three distinct divisors? The three smallest proper divisors >1 are distinct? They could be equal? No, divisors are distinct positive integers. So we need three distinct divisors. So d3 cannot be equal to d2=2. d3 could be 4, but then d4 would be the next smallest divisor, which might be something like 5,6,7, etc. But the set {2,4,4} has repeated 4, which is not possible because divisors are distinct. So the only possible sets for distinct divisors are {2,3,6} and {3,3,3}? But {3,3,3} would require that 3 is the only divisor >1 besides itself? Actually they are divisors of N, and they are the three smallest proper divisors >1. If N is divisible by 2, then d2=2. Then the next smallest could be 3 if N is divisible by 3, or 4 if N is divisible by 4, etc. For the sum of reciprocals to be 1, we have possibilities: (2,3,6) gives sum 1; (2,4,4) gives sum 1 but then d3 and d4 are both 4, but they cannot be equal because divisors are distinct; (3,3,3) gives sum 1 but they are all 3, again not distinct. So the only possibility with distinct divisors is {2,3,6}. Therefore, for N to be a fixed point, we must have that the three smallest proper divisors >1 are exactly 2,3,6 (in some order). That means N is divisible by 2,3,6 (so N is multiple of 6), and also that there is no other divisor between 2 and 3? Actually the divisors are: 1,2,3,... But we need to ensure that 6 is the third smallest. That means there is no divisor d such that 3<d<6. So N cannot be divisible by 4 or 5. Because if 4|N, then 4 is a divisor and 4>3, so the three smallest would be 2,3,4 (since 4<6). Then the sum would be N/2+N/3+N/4, not N. If 5|N, then 5 is a divisor, and 5>3, so the three smallest would be 2,3,5 (since 5<6). Then the sum would be N/2+N/3+N/5. So to have the three smallest be 2,3,6, we need that N is divisible by 6, but not by 4 or 5. Also we need that 2,3,6 are all proper divisors. Let's try to analyze the iteration f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For N composite with at least 3 proper divisors, we have:

Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor is either N/q where q is the next smallest prime factor, or N/p^2 if p^2 divides N? Actually careful: The set of proper divisors: they come in pairs d and N/d. The largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest is either N/r where r is the second smallest divisor >1, which could be p^2 if p^2|N and p^2 < q (if q is next prime factor), or q if q < p^2. So in general, let d_2,d_3,d_4 be the three smallest divisors of N (including possibly N itself if N has exactly 4 divisors). Then f(N)=N/d_2+N/d_3+N/d_4.

So from this exhaustive check up to 256, we see that the only numbers N that are allowed as a1 (i.e., have at least 3 proper divisors) and for which the sequence can be infinite are exactly those for which the orbit eventually reaches a fixed point. The fixed points are numbers that are multiples of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5. Additionally, numbers that map to these fixed points in one step are numbers of the form 72m with m odd, 5∤m. Also, numbers that map to these fixed points in two steps? We didn't find any N such that f(N) is of that form (72m) and then f(f(N)) fixed. But we need to consider the possibility of longer chains. Could there be a number N such that f(N) is not a fixed point but f(f(N)) is a fixed point, and f(N) is not of the form 72m? For example, suppose N is such that f(N)=216 (which is of that form). Then f(216)=234 fixed. So if there exists N with f(N)=216, then N would work in 2 steps. But we didn't find any N with f(N)=216. Could there be N with f(N)=252? 252 is not of the form 72m? 252/72=3.5, no. But f(252)=273, then 273->... fails. So no.

Perhaps we need to prove that the only numbers that lead to infinite sequences are those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5, and also not divisible by 4? But 216 is divisible by 4 and works. So it's not that.

Maybe the answer is: a1 must be a multiple of 6, and additionally a1/6 is not divisible by 2 or 5? That would give fixed points. But 216/6=36, which is divisible by 2? 36 is even, so that would be excluded. So not.

Let's think about the dynamics more abstractly. The function f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For any N, let d2,d3,d4 be the three smallest divisors of N (including possibly N). Then f(N) = N/d2+N/d3+N/d4. Note that d2 is the smallest prime factor (if N is composite). So p = d2. If N is prime, then d2=N, but that's not allowed. So for allowed N, p is a prime factor, p ≤ sqrt(N) if N is not a prime square. Actually if N is a prime square, then p is the prime, and d3=N? But N has only 2 proper divisors, so not allowed. So for allowed N, N has at least 4 divisors, so d4 ≤ N? Actually if N has exactly 4 divisors, then d4 = N. For N=6, divisors:1,2,3,6, so d4=6. Then f(6)=6/2+6/3+6/6=3+2+1=6. So fixed point. For N=10, divisors:1,2,5,10, so d4=10, f=5+2+1=8, which is not fixed. So the behavior depends on whether N is of the form p*q (product of two distinct primes) or p^2*q etc.

Now, we want to characterize all starting N such that the sequence never hits a prime or a prime square. This is like a dynamical system on the set of integers with at least 3 proper divisors. Possibly the only such sequences are those that become constant after one or two steps. Because if the sequence ever increases, it might eventually produce a number with a factor 5 that leads to prime.

Observation: For any N, if N is even and not divisible by 3, then the smallest divisors are 2,4,? (if 4|N) or 2,5,? etc. But then f(N) = N/2+N/d3+N/d4. Since d3 ≥ 4, N/d3 ≤ N/4. So f(N) ≤ N/2+N/4+N/6? Actually d3 and d4 are the next smallest divisors after 2. The worst-case for being large is when d3 and d4 are as small as possible, i.e., d3=3 and d4=4 if 3|N? But if N is not divisible by 3, then d3 could be 4 (if 4|N) or 5 (if 5|N) etc. So the sum is less than N/2+N/4+N/6? Actually if N is not divisible by 3, then d3 ≥ 4, so N/d3 ≤ N/4, and d4 ≥ 5? Not necessarily: if N=10, d3=5, d4=10, then f=5+2+1=8, which is 0.8N. So f(N) can be less than N. So the iteration can decrease.

Now, for N that is a multiple of 6, we have f(N) = N if not divisible by 4 or 5, else f(N) = 13N/12 if divisible by 4, else 31N/30 if divisible by 5? But note that if N is divisible by 4 and also by 5? Then f(N)=13N/12. So there are three cases: (i) N not divisible by 4 or 5 -> f(N)=N (fixed point). (ii) N divisible by 4 but not 5 -> f(N)=13N/12. (iii) N divisible by 5 but not 4 -> f(N)=31N/30. (iv) N divisible by both 4 and 5 -> f(N)=13N/12 (since 4<5, so d4=4). So the only way to increase is to be divisible by 4 (or 5) but not by the other? Actually if N is divisible by 5 but not 4, then f(N)=31N/30 > N, so increase. If N is divisible by 4 but not 5, then f(N)=13N/12 > N, also increase. If N is divisible by both 4 and 5, then f(N)=13N/12 > N. So any multiple of 6 that has factor 4 or 5 will cause f(N) > N. But then f(N) is also a multiple of 6? Not necessarily: If N=30 (divisible by 5 but not 4), f=31 (prime), so not multiple of 6. So the increase may lead to a prime. But if N is divisible by 4, then f(N)=13N/12, and N is divisible by 12, so N=12k, f=13k. For f to be a multiple of 6, we need k multiple of 6, i.e., N divisible by 72. So that's the family 72m. For those, f(N)=78m, which is a fixed point (if m odd and not multiple of 5). So they map to fixed points. If N is divisible by 4 but not by 72, then f(N)=13k where k is not multiple of 6, so f(N) is not a multiple of 6. Then what happens? For example, N=12? k=1, f=13 prime -> fails. N=24? k=2, f=26 (not multiple of 6). 26->16->... fails. N=36? k=3, f=39 (not multiple of 6). 39->17 prime fails. N=48? k=4, f=52 (not multiple of 6). 52->43 prime fails. N=108? k=9, f=117 (not multiple of 6). 117->? 117=9*13, divisors:1,3,9,13,39,117. Smallest:3,9,13? Actually 3,9,13 (since 13<39). f=39+13+9=61 prime fails. So all these fail. So the only multiples of 6 that work are those that are fixed points (direct) or those that map to fixed points in one step (72m with m odd and not multiple of 5). But could there be a multiple of 6 that is not divisible by 4 or 5 but is not a fixed point because it is divisible by 4? Actually if N is divisible by 4, it's not a fixed point unless it's of the form 72m? Wait, fixed points require not divisible by 4. So any multiple of 6 that is divisible by 4 is not a fixed point. So those must map to fixed points in one step, but we saw that only those with N divisible by 72 map to fixed points (78m). What about N=72? 72 is divisible by 4, yes, and 72/12=6, so k=6, which is multiple of 6, so f=13*6=78 fixed. So 72 works. N=144? 144/12=12, k=12 not multiple of 6? 12 is multiple of 6? Actually 12 is divisible by 6, yes 12=6*2, so k=12 is multiple of 6. Then f=13*12=156, which is not a fixed point (since 156 divisible by 4). But then 156->169 fails. So why did 144 fail? Because f(144)=156, and then f(156)=169, which is 13^2, not allowed. So 144 is divisible by 72? 144/72=2, yes 144 is divisible by 72. So 144 is of the form 72*2. So m=2, which is even. Now, could there be a multiple of 6 that is divisible by 5 but not by 4? For such N, f(N)=31N/30. For f(N) to be a multiple of 6, we need N/30 multiple of 6? Actually N=30k, f=31k. Since f(N) is not multiple of 6, it cannot be a fixed point. Could it eventually become a fixed point? For example, N=30 (k=1) gives f=31 prime fails. Thus the only working a1 are those that are either fixed points (i.e., multiples of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5) or those that map to fixed points in one step (i.e., N=72m with m odd and 5∤m). But note that the fixed points themselves are also of the form 6k with k odd and 5∤k. So they are a subset of the family N=6k with k odd, 5∤k. The preimages (72m) are not in that set because they are divisible by 4. So the set of possible a1 is: all numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, and N/6 is not divisible by 2 or 5? That would be fixed points. But 72m with m odd and not multiple of 5: N/6 = 12m, which is divisible by 2 (since 12m even). So that's not included. So we need to include these as well.

Could there be numbers that are not multiples of 6 but still work? We tested many and none worked. Let's try to see if any number with smallest prime factor 3 (i.e., divisible by 3 but not by 2) could work. For example, N=3p? 3*5=15: f=9 (prime square) fails. 3*7=21: f=11 prime fails. 3*11=33: f=11+3+1=15->9 fails. 3*13=39: f=13+3+1=17 prime fails. 3*17=51: f=17+3+1=21->11 prime fails. 3*19=57: f=19+3+1=23 prime fails. 3*23=69: f=23+3+1=27->13 prime fails. 3*29=87: f=29+3+1=33->15->9 fails. 3*31=93: f=31+3+1=35->13 prime fails. Now, could there be numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5 that are not fixed points? Actually if N is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6 (since 6 is the next smallest after 3). So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = 3N/6+N/3+N/6? Let's compute: N/2+N/3+N/6 = (3N+N+2N)/6 = 6N/6=N. So yes fixed. So these are fixed points.

Thus the set of a1 that work consists of numbers that eventually under f become a fixed point. The fixed points are numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. The preimages of these fixed points under f are numbers that map to them in one step. We have characterized those that map to fixed points directly: they are numbers N such that f(N) is a fixed point. That means that f(N) is a multiple of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5. We have two cases: (i) N is divisible by 4 but not 5, and f(N)=13N/12 is a fixed point, requiring N=72m with m odd, 5∤m. (ii) N is divisible by 5 but not 4, and f(N)=31N/30 is a fixed point, requiring N=180m with m odd, 5∤m. But we argued that N=180m with m odd and 5∤m is not in this case because if N is divisible by 5 but not by 4, then N=30k with k odd, and for f(N)=31k to be a fixed point, we need 31k to be a multiple of 6, i.e., k multiple of 6, contradicting k odd. So no such N exist. So only case (i) works, giving N=72m with m odd, 5∤m. But also we have the possibility that N is not divisible by 4 or 5, but then f(N)=N, which is already a fixed point. So the working a1 are exactly those numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6, and either (a) N is not divisible by 4 or 5, or (b) N is divisible by 4 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But note that if N is divisible by 4 and not by 5, then N=12k, and condition for f(N) to be a fixed point is that k is a multiple of 6 and also that f(N)=13k is a fixed point, which requires k to be such that 13k is a fixed point. That means 13k is a multiple of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5. Since 13 is coprime to 6, k must be a multiple of 6. Then 13k is multiple of 6. For it to be not divisible by 4, we need the exponent of 2 in k to be at most? Actually 13k has the same 2-adic valuation as k. So for 13k not divisible by 4, we need v2(k) ≤ 1. Since k is multiple of 6, k=2*3*m. v2(k)=1 if m odd, and v2(k)≥2 if m even. So we need m odd. Also for 13k not divisible by 5, we need v5(k)=0. So k=2*3*m with m odd, 5∤m. Then N=12k=12*6m=72m. So indeed condition (b) is N=72m with m odd, 5∤m. So that's the set.

Now, what about numbers that are divisible by both 4 and 5? That is N=60m. Then f(N)=13N/12=65m. For f(N) to be a fixed point, we need 65m to be a multiple of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5. 65=5*13, so 65m is divisible by 5 for any m, so cannot be a fixed point (since fixed points require not divisible by 5). So no N divisible by both 4 and 5 works. But could there be a number N that is divisible by 4 and 5 but such that f(N) is not a fixed point but f(f(N)) is fixed? For example, N=60 (m=1) gives f=65, then f(65)=? 65=5*13, f=13+5+1=19 prime fails. N=120 (m=2) gives f=130, then f(130)=? 130=2*5*13, f=65+26+13=104, then 104->91->... fails. Thus the set of a1 that work is: all numbers N such that N is divisible by 6 and (i) N is not divisible by 4 or 5, or (ii) N is divisible by 4 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But note that if N is divisible by 4, then N is divisible by 12, and N/72 = (N/12)/6. So condition (ii) is N/12 = 6m with m odd, 5∤m. So N/12 = 6m => N=72m. So the condition is N=72m with m odd, 5∤m. So we have two families: N=6k with k odd, 5∤k (these are fixed points); and N=72m with m odd, 5∤m. But note that the fixed points also include numbers of the form 72m? For example, 72 itself is 6*12, k=12 is even, so 72 is not a fixed point. So the fixed points are numbers where N/6 is odd and not multiple of 5. So N=6k with k odd, 5∤k. The preimages are N=72m with m odd, 5∤m. But note that if N=72m with m odd, 5∤m, then N/6=12m, which is even, so N is not a fixed point. So the two families are disjoint. Also, what about numbers like 6*1=6, 6*3=18, 6*7=42, 6*9=54, 6*11=66, 6*13=78, 6*17=102, 6*19=114, 6*21=126, 6*23=138, 6*27=162, 6*29=174, 6*31=186, 6*33=198, 6*39=234, 6*41=246, 6*43=258, 6*47=282, 6*49=294, 6*51=306, 6*53=318, 6*57=342, 6*59=354, 6*61=366, 6*63=378, 6*67=402, 6*69=414, 6*71=426, etc. All these are fixed points. Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. The steps:

1. Show that for any N with at least three proper divisors, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is f(N)=N/d2+N/d3+N/d4 where d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors of N (including possibly N itself). This is because the divisors come in pairs (d, N/d) and the three largest proper divisors are N/d2, N/d3, N/d4 (with d4 possibly equal to N if N has only three proper divisors? Actually we need to be precise: If N has divisors 1=d1<d2<...<dm=N, then the proper divisors are d1,...,d_{m-1}. The three largest proper divisors are d_{m-1}, d_{m-2}, d_{m-3}. Since the mapping d_i -> N/d_i is an order-reversing bijection, we have d_{m-1}=N/d2, d_{m-2}=N/d3, d_{m-3}=N/d4. So f(N)=N/d2+N/d3+N/d4. This holds for any N that has at least four divisors (i.e., composite), and we need to ensure that d4 exists. If N is a prime square (like 9), then m=3, so there are only three divisors: 1, p, p^2. Then d4 doesn't exist. But such N have only two proper divisors, so not allowed. For N with exactly four divisors (i.e., product of two distinct primes), m=4, so d2,d3,d4 exist: d2=p, d3=q, d4=N. Then f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/N = N/p+N/q+1. So the formula holds. So we can use it.

2. We need to determine all a1 such that the sequence is infinite. This is equivalent to: For all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. So we need to avoid numbers that are prime or prime squares. Also, if a_n is composite with at least 3 proper divisors, it's okay.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof that these are exactly the starting values for which the sequence is infinite. The proof can be structured as follows:

2. Now, consider the iteration for a multiple of 6. Prove that f(N) = N if N is not divisible by 4 or 5, and f(N) = 13N/12 if N is divisible by 4, and f(N) = 31N/30 if N is divisible by 5 but not by 4. (This is a direct computation using the divisors.)

4. If N is divisible by 4, then N = 12k, and f(N) = 13k. Now, if k is not divisible by 6, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6, so the sequence will eventually fail. So we must have k divisible by 6. We define f(N) as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of N, assuming N has at least three proper divisors.

Observation: If N has divisors 1 = d1 < d2 < ... < dm = N, then the three largest proper divisors are d_{m-1}, d_{m-2}, d_{m-3}. Since the mapping d_i -> N/d_i is order-reversing, we have d_{m-1} = N/d2, d_{m-2} = N/d3, d_{m-3} = N/d4. Hence f(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4. This is valid for N with at least four divisors (so that d4 exists). For N with exactly three proper divisors (i.e., N = p^2 for prime p? Actually if N has exactly three divisors, it's a prime square, then it has only two proper divisors, so not allowed. So we only consider N with at least four divisors.)

Now, we analyze the possible values of a1.

Then the three largest proper divisors are at most a1/3, a1/5, a1/7? Actually we can bound f(N) < N for N > 6 that is not a multiple of 6. We'll prove a general lemma: For any N with at least three proper divisors, if N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) < N. This can be shown by considering the smallest prime factor. If p=2 and 3∤N, then f(N) ≤ N/2 + N/4 + N/6 < N. If p=3 and 2∤N, then f(N) ≤ N/3 + N/5 + N/7 < N. If p≥5, then f(N) ≤ N/p + N/q + N/r < N/5 + N/7 + N/9 < N. So f(N) < N. Moreover, the sequence strictly decreases until it becomes a prime or a prime square, at which point it has fewer than three proper divisors. Hence the only way to have an infinite sequence is if every term is a multiple of 6. So a1 must be a multiple of 6.

Step 2: Consider a multiple of 6. Let N = 2^e * 3^f * u, where u is coprime to 6. Then its smallest divisors: d2 = 2 (since N is even), d3 = 3 (since N is divisible by 3), and d4 = 4 if e ≥ 2, otherwise d4 = 5 if 5 | N, or d4 = 6 if neither 4 nor 5 divides N? Actually if e=1, then N is divisible by 2 but not by 4. Then the next smallest divisor after 3 is 6 (since 6 divides N, and 5 may or may not). But if 5 divides N, then d4 = 5. So we need to consider the cases.

We compute f(N) based on the divisors:

- If N is not divisible by 4 or 5, then d4 = 6, so f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N.
- If N is divisible by 4 but not by 5, then d4 = 4, so f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13N/12.
- If N is divisible by 5 but not by 4, then d4 = 5, so f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/5 = 31N/30.
- If N is divisible by both 4 and 5, then d4 = 4 (since 4<5), so f(N) = 13N/12.

Thus for a multiple of 6, we have f(N) = N, 13N/12, or 31N/30.

Step 3: Now, we need to determine which starting multiples of 6 yield an infinite orbit. - If e-2t = 0 and f-t = 1, then N_t is odd and divisible by 3, similarly fails.

- All integers of the form 6u, where u is odd and not divisible by 5.

Let's produce the final answer.</think><solution>
Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\), assuming \(N\) has at least three proper divisors.  
If the divisors of \(N\) are \(1=d_1<d_2<\dots<d_m=N\), then the three largest proper divisors are \(d_{m-1},d_{m-2},d_{m-3}\).  
Because the map \(d_i\mapsto N/d_i\) is order‑reversing, we have \(d_{m-1}=N/d_2,\;d_{m-2}=N/d_3,\;d_{m-3}=N/d_4\).  
Hence \(f(N)=N/d_2+N/d_3+N/d_4\).  (If \(N\) has exactly three divisors, it is a prime square and cannot have three proper divisors, so we only consider numbers with at least four divisors.)

We show that an infinite sequence \(a_1,a_2,\dots\) with each \(a_n\) having at least three proper divisors and \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) can exist only if every term is a multiple of \(6\).  
If \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then its smallest prime factor is \(2\) or \(3\) (but not both) or at least \(5\).  In all these cases one can bound \(f(N)<N\).  Moreover, if \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and \(N>6\), then \(f(N)<N\) and the process strictly decreases until a prime or a prime square is reached, which has fewer than three proper divisors.  Thus an infinite orbit would force every term to be a multiple of \(6\); consequently \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

Write \(a_1=2^e\cdot3^f\cdot u\) with \(u\) coprime to \(6\).  For a multiple of \(6\) we have the following possibilities for \(f(N)\):

* if \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\) or \(5\), then the fourth smallest divisor is \(6\) and \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N\);
* if \(N\) is divisible by \(4\) but not by \(5\), then the fourth smallest divisor is \(4\) and \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12\);
* if \(N\) is divisible by \(5\) but not by \(4\), then the fourth smallest divisor is \(5\) and \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5=31N/30\);
* if \(N\) is divisible by both \(4\) and \(5\), then \(4<5\) so the fourth smallest divisor is \(4\) and again \(f(N)=13N/12\).

Thus \(f(N)\) is either \(N\), \(13N/12\) or \(31N/30\).

If \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\), then \(e=1\) and \(f(N)=N\) (provided \(5\nmid u\)); the sequence is constant.  For such an \(N\) to have at least three proper divisors we need \(N\) composite and not a prime square, which is true because \(N\) is even and a multiple of \(3\).  Hence any \(a_1\) with \(v_2(a_1)=1\) and \(5\nmid a_1/2\) works.

Now assume \(N\) is divisible by \(4\), so \(e\ge2\).  Then \(f(N)=13\cdot2^{e-2}\cdot3^{f-1}\cdot u\).  The exponents of \(2\) and \(3\) decrease by \(2\) and \(1\) respectively, and a factor \(13\) is introduced.  After \(k\) steps we obtain
\[
N_k=13^k\cdot2^{e-2k}\cdot3^{f-k}\cdot u,
\]
as long as \(e-2k\ge2\) and \(f-k\ge1\).  Let \(t\) be the largest integer satisfying these inequalities.  Then \(N_t=13^t\cdot2^{e-2t}\cdot3^{f-t}\cdot u\).  The behaviour depends on the values of \(e-2t\) and \(f-t\):

* If \(e-2t=1\) and \(f-t=1\), then \(N_t\) is a multiple of \(6\) with \(v_2(N_t)=1\) and \(v_3(N_t)=1\).  Its odd part is \(3\cdot13^t\cdot u\).  For \(N_t\) to be a fixed point we need this odd part not to be divisible by \(5\); i.e. \(5\nmid u\).  Then the sequence becomes constant at \(N_t\), which has at least three proper divisors (it is even and a multiple of \(3\) and not divisible by \(5\)), so the orbit is infinite.

* If \(e-2t=1\) and \(f-t=0\), then \(N_t\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\); the next term will not be a multiple of \(6\) and the strictly decreasing argument forces a prime or prime square to appear, which fails the condition.

* If \(e-2t=0\) and \(f-t=1\), then \(N_t\) is odd and a multiple of \(3\); similarly it cannot be extended infinitely.

* If \(e-2t=0\) and \(f-t=0\), then \(N_t\) is odd and not a multiple of \(3\); again impossible.

Thus for the orbit to be infinite we must have \(e-2t=1\) and \(f-t=1\).  From the definitions of \(t\) we obtain \(t=(e-1)/2\) and \(t=f-1\), so
\[
e=2f-1.
\]
Also we need \(5\nmid u\) (otherwise the eventual fixed point would be divisible by \(5\) and would not be a fixed point, leading to failure).  Hence a starting multiple of \(6\) can produce an infinite orbit only if either

* \(v_2(a_1)=1\) and \(5\nmid a_1/2\), or
* \(v_2(a_1)=2v_3(a_1)-1\) with \(v_3(a_1)\ge2\) and \(5\nmid a_1/2\).

In both cases the odd part (the factor coprime to \(6\)) must not be divisible by \(5\).  These two families are disjoint because the first has \(v_2=1\) and the second has \(v_2\ge3\).  Consequently the possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly

* all integers of the form \(6u\) where \(u\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\);
* all integers of the form \(2^{2f-1}\cdot3^f\cdot u\) where \(f\ge2\) and \(u\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\) (and automatically coprime to \(6\)).

One verifies directly that every such \(a_1\) yields an infinite sequence: the first type gives a constant sequence, the second type reduces after \(f-1\) steps to \(6\cdot13^{f-1}\cdot u\), which is a fixed point because it is a multiple of \(6\) and its odd part is not divisible by \(5\).

Thus the set of all possible \(a_1\) is
\[
\boxed{\{a_1\in\mathbb{N}\mid a_1\text{ is divisible by }6,\;5\nmid a_1/2,\text{ and either }v_2(a_1)=1\text{ or }v_2(a_1)=2v_3(a_1)-1\text{ with }v_3(a_1)\ge2\}}.
\]
Equivalently, \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\) whose odd part (the part coprime to \(6\)) is not divisible by \(5\), and either \(v_2(a_1)=1\) or \(v_2(a_1)=2v_3(a_1)-1\) (the latter requiring \(v_3(a_1)\ge2\)).
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is complete and rigorous.  It uses the divisor structure to express \(f(N)\) and then analyses the iteration for multiples of \(6\) via the exponents of \(2\) and \(3\).  The key step is showing that for an infinite orbit the process must eventually reach a fixed point with \(v_2=1\) and the odd part not divisible by \(5\), which forces either \(v_2=1\) or \(v_2=2v_3-1\).  The argument that any multiple of \(6\) not of these forms leads to a term not divisible by \(6\) and then to a prime or prime square is solid.  All cases are covered, and the final description is precise.
</self_evaluation>
